SpaceX Jumps in First Day Following Record $75B IPO | The Close 6/12/202692 min34 insights2026-06-12
▶ Watch on YouTube
Bloomberg's The Close covers SpaceX's record $75B IPO, which closed up 19% at a ~$2.1T market cap. Experts debate whether the fixed-price deal left ~$20B on the table, whether a sum-of-parts valuation justifies the price, the extreme single-class governance that forces index funds to own it, and whether the entire orbital-data-center thesis hinges on Starship working.
IPO Mechanics & Money Left on the Table · 5Valuation Debate: Limited Upside vs Sum-of-Parts · 6Governance, Index Inclusion & Forced Ownership · 6IPO Supply Wave & Market Absorption · 5Execution Risk: Starship as the Swing Factor · 4Market Internals & Space-Sector Rotation · 5Space Economy & Direct-to-Retail Capital Raising · 3
IPO Mechanics & Money Left on the Table 5
SpaceX used an unusual fixed-price IPO at $135/share set before the roadshow even started, rather than the standard bookbuilt price range.
data_point🗣 Jay Ritter (University of Florida IPO professor)conviction: highSpaceXElon Musk
So what: A fixed price set pre-roadshow removes price discovery and signals issuer confidence/control; investors should expect larger first-day pops and use the structure as a template-breaker, not a market norm.
“THE FIXED-PRICE SETTING THE OFFER PRICE EVEN BEFORE THE ROADSHOW STARTED IS VERY UNUSUAL. THE JUMP TODAY IS NOT AT ALL UNUSUAL.” ▶ 4:40
At its first-day trading level, SpaceX left roughly $20 billion on the table as first-day profit to allocated buyers, more than twice the prior record of ~$9 billion set by Alibaba in 2014.
data_point🗣 Jay Ritter (University of Florida IPO professor)conviction: highSpaceXAlibaba
So what: The record underpricing means allocated institutions captured an enormous wealth transfer; signals the deal could have priced higher and rewards anyone who secured an allocation.
“$20 BILLION OF MONEY LEFT ON THE TABLE WAS MORE THAN TWICE THAT OF ANY OTHER U.S. IPO.” ▶ 5:14
SpaceX's planned retail allocation was cut from a reported 30% to about 20%, and Robinhood retail accounts that requested shares received only one share each.
data_point🗣 Jay Ritter (University of Florida IPO professor)conviction: mediumSpaceXRobinhoodTesla
So what: Tiny retail fills mean pent-up retail demand remains unsatisfied, a potential source of continued post-IPO buying pressure on the stock.
“MY FRIENDS WHO HAVE A ROBINHOOD ACCOUNT AND ASKED FOR SHARES HAVE EACH RECEIVED ONE SHARE EACH.” ▶ 6:29
SpaceX traded ~483-500 million shares on its first day, the biggest volume mover of the session, roughly 50% above the ~282 million shares traded in Tesla's 2010 IPO.
data_point🗣 Romaine (Bloomberg anchor)conviction: highSpaceXTeslaNasdaq
So what: Record secondary-market liquidity on day one shows the market absorbed the largest IPO ever smoothly, de-risking the near-term liquidity concern for follow-on mega-IPOs.
SpaceX opened for trading around 11:47am, taking roughly two hours from the bell, within the normal one-to-three-hour window for large Nasdaq/NYSE debuts.
data_point🗣 Bloomberg Nasdaq floor reporterconviction: mediumSpaceXNasdaq
So what: An orderly open with no auction breakdown signals stabilization agents and market makers handled record size well, a positive read for the pipeline of giant IPOs to follow.
Valuation Debate: Limited Upside vs Sum-of-Parts 6
Because SpaceX is already worth ~$2.1 trillion, even matching Nvidia (the world's most valuable company at roughly twice that) implies only ~100% upside, far less headroom than Nvidia itself had when it IPO'd in 1999 at a ~$600M cap and jumped 60% on day one.
framework🗣 Jay Ritter (University of Florida IPO professor)conviction: highSpaceXNvidia
So what: Buying a company already priced near the top of the global market cap leaderboard structurally caps return potential; a reason for valuation-sensitive investors to stay cautious despite the pop.
“WHAT IF SPACEX IS INCREDIBLY SUCCESSFUL AND BECOMES AS VALUABLE AS THE WORLD'S MOST VALUABLE COMPANY, NVIDIA? THAT IS ONLY A 100% UPSIDE POTENTIAL. THAT IS NOT A LOT.” ▶ 7:43
A sum-of-parts model values SpaceX at ~$1.8 trillion using the highest peer multiples across three segments: ~$1.2T for Starlink connectivity, ~$400B for space launch, and ~$200-300B for the AI business.
framework🗣 PitchBook senior research analystconviction: highSpaceXStarlinkStarship
So what: The fundamental fair value sits below the ~$2.1T trading cap even on generous multiples, implying the market is paying an Elon/AI premium of ~$300B+ over a parts-based valuation.
“YOU TAKE THE HIGHEST MULTIPLE OF THE PEER GROUPS ACROSS EACH OF THE THREE SEGMENTS, YOU GET TO A VALUATION OF $1.8 TRILLION.” ▶ 20:25
Starlink, not launch, is the dominant value driver in SpaceX's valuation, accounting for roughly two-thirds of a sum-of-parts estimate.
thesis🗣 PitchBook senior research analystconviction: highSpaceXStarlink
So what: Investors framing SpaceX as a 'rocket company' or an 'AI company' are mis-weighting it; the connectivity/satellite-broadband business is what the price is really discounting.
SpaceX's xAI-driven AI segment could be running at roughly $30 billion in annualized AI revenue, but as a largely infrastructure/neo-cloud business it likely cannot command the multiple of frontier-model peers like OpenAI and Anthropic.
causal🗣 PitchBook senior research analystconviction: mediumSpaceXxAIOpenAIAnthropic
So what: The market is pricing the AI unit as if it earns frontier-model multiples; if it's really infrastructure, that premium is at risk of compressing.
“I'M NOT SURE IF THE AI BUSINESS FROM SPACEX WILL BE ABLE TO GET THAT TYPE OF MULTIPLE, GIVEN THAT MOST OF THE REVENUE WILL BE INFRASTRUCTURE AND NOT THE FRONTIER MODEL.” ▶ 23:39
Unlike OpenAI and Anthropic, SpaceX is a 24-year-old cash-flow-generative business whose Starlink unit is a cash cow funding the incremental AI/xAI buildout, rather than a cash-burning startup.
thesis🗣 Daniel Hanson (Neuberger Berman)conviction: highSpaceXStarlinkxAIOpenAIAnthropic
So what: The self-funding profile differentiates SpaceX from other AI-era mega-IPOs and supports a quality-investor case that it can scale AI without external capital dependence.
“STARLINK IS A CASH COW. THOSE CASH FLOWS ARE GOING TO BE FUNDING THE INCREMENTAL GROWTH AROUND X AI.” ▶ 1:02:04
The motivation for taking SpaceX public, announced by Musk on December 10, was specifically to raise capital for the AI/data-center opportunity rather than for launch or Starlink, which are already self-sustaining.
causal🗣 Daniel Hanson (Neuberger Berman)conviction: mediumSpaceXElon MuskxAI
So what: Use-of-proceeds signals capital will flow to compute/data centers, so investors should track SpaceX's AI capex and monetization, not just rocket milestones.
“THE MOTIVATION FOR ANNOUNCING THE ASPIRATION FOR AN IPO IS TO RAISE ASSETS TO GO AFTER THE A.I. OPPORTUNITY AND ULTIMATELY DATA CENTERS.” ▶ 1:02:23
Governance, Index Inclusion & Forced Ownership 6
SpaceX's IPO is a one-off driven by the 'cult of Elon' and should not be read as a signal for the broader IPO market given its scale, unusual governance, and apparently light-touch SEC review.
contrarian🗣 Lise Buyer (Class V Group, Google IPO architect)conviction: highSpaceXElon MuskSEC
So what: Investors extrapolating a SpaceX 'halo effect' to other IPOs are likely wrong; the deal's terms won't be repeatable, so don't price comparable deals off it.
“I DON'T THINK WE CAN READ ANYTHING ABOUT THE REST OF THE IPO MARKET FROM THIS PARTICULAR TRANSACTION... THIS IS A ONE-OFF.” ▶ 26:49
SpaceX represents an extreme dual-class/governance case where new shareholders have essentially no recourse except selling, with no independent board and no sunset provisions, and this structure is unlikely to be accepted by investors at most other companies.
thesis🗣 Lise Buyer / Mark Levine (NYC Comptroller)conviction: highSpaceXElon MuskGoogleMeta
So what: Governance risk is uniquely high; institutions buy only because of indexing, and the lack of guardrails is a tail risk if execution falters.
“WE HAVE NEVER SEEN A MAJOR COMPANY WHERE A SINGLE PERSON HAS THIS MUCH OF A STRANGLEHOLD ON GOVERNANCE WITH VERY LIMITED SHAREHOLDER RIGHTS, NO INDEPENDENT BOARD, AND NO SUNSET TO ANY OF THIS.” ▶ 1:13:40
FTSE Russell is fast-tracking SpaceX into the Russell 3000, bypassing normal seasoning criteria, which could force NYC pension funds to own roughly $1.5 billion of the stock by the end of the week regardless of governance objections.
data_point🗣 Mark Levine (NYC Comptroller)conviction: highSpaceXFTSE RussellRussell 3000NYC pension funds
So what: Passive index mechanics create non-discretionary, valuation-insensitive buying of SpaceX; a structural bid that supports the stock near-term but removes investor choice.
“THEY ARE GOING TO FAST-TRACK SPACEX. THEY ARE GOING TO THROW AWAY ALL THE NORMAL CRITERIA, AND WE COULD POTENTIALLY OWN ONE POINT 5 BILLION OF SPACEX BY THE END OF THE WEEK.” ▶ 1:12:13
SpaceX enters the Nasdaq 100 in about 15 trading days, with FTSE/Russell fast-tracking sooner, while S&P 500 inclusion is being held off for about a year as S&P adjusts its methodology.
data_point🗣 Katie Greifeld (Bloomberg anchor)conviction: highSpaceXNasdaq 100S&P 500FTSE Russell
So what: Staggered index inclusion creates a sequence of forced-buying events; the Nasdaq 100 add is a near-term catalyst and the S&P delay means benchmarks will diverge for ~a year.
Over $2 trillion in collective assets across dozens of pension funds and investors signed a letter to the exchanges objecting to SpaceX's governance and fast-track index inclusion.
data_point🗣 Mark Levine (NYC Comptroller)conviction: highSpaceXpension funds
So what: Coordinated fiduciary pushback signals a coming governance/activism fight once index funds become large holders, a potential overhang on the inclusion narrative.
Founder-led tech companies with dual-class structures have historically outperformed non-dual-class peers over their first 10 years as public companies, though SpaceX is described as taking control to an extreme.
framework🗣 Lise Buyer (Class V Group, Google IPO architect)conviction: mediumSpaceXGoogleTesla
So what: The historical dual-class outperformance gives bulls a data-backed defense of Musk's control, but only 'with limits' — SpaceX's no-sunset structure tests the thesis.
“THOSE THAT ARE FOUNDER-LED, HAVE TENDED TO OUTPERFORM THOSE THAT DID NOT HAVE A DUAL CLASS FOR THE FIRST 10 YEARS AS A PUBLIC COMPANY.” ▶ 30:34
IPO Supply Wave & Market Absorption 5
The SpaceX IPO presages a sizable wave of IPOs and shows capital markets are willing to finance the AI infrastructure and space build-out, per Goldman Sachs' president.
prediction🗣 Goldman Sachs president (John Waldron, transcribed as 'Bolton')conviction: highSpaceXGoldman Sachs
So what: Sell-side leadership is signaling a reopened IPO/financing window for AI and space, a tailwind for underwriters and a pipeline of OpenAI/Anthropic-style deals.
≈ “IT PRESAGES THE BEGINNING OF A SIZABLE WAVE OF IPO'S... CAPITAL MARKETS ARE DEMONSTRATING A WILLINGNESS TO FINANCE THIS AI INFRASTRUCTURE BUILD AND THIS BUILD IN SPACE.” paraphrase ▶ 2:47
2026 is set to break records for IPOs and secondaries, with SpaceX kicking it off followed by OpenAI and Anthropic, as supply finally meets demand after the dry 2023-2025 window.
prediction🗣 Gene Munster (Deepwater, referred to as 'Gino's company')conviction: highSpaceXOpenAIAnthropic
So what: A record issuance year reverses years of shrinking equity supply; investors should prepare for a flood of mega-cap private names hitting public markets.
“WE ARE NOW GOING TO BREAK RECORDS IN 2026 FOR IPO'S AND SECONDARIES. SPACEX IS THE GRANDDADDY KICKING IT ALL OFF.” ▶ 19:01
U.S. net equity supply is expected to be roughly flat in 2026 after several years of negative net new supply, as mega-cap tech names like Google reverse buybacks to issue equity.
data_point🗣 Julie Biel (Kayne Anderson Rudnick)conviction: highGoogleAlphabet
So what: The end of the negative-net-supply tailwind that lifted equities removes a structural support; flat-to-rising share supply is a headwind for index returns.
“IT'S EXPECTED TO BE FLAT IN 2026. WE ARE COMING OFF OF SEVERAL YEARS WHERE THERE'S BEEN NEGATIVE NET NEW SUPPLY OF EQUITIES.” ▶ 43:06
Google reversing its consistent ~2.5%/year share-count decline to do an equity raise looks like a competitive move to starve rivals of capital rather than a financing necessity.
contrarian🗣 Julie Biel (Kayne Anderson Rudnick)conviction: mediumGoogleAlphabet
So what: If well-capitalized tech giants issue equity to crowd out younger AI upstarts, it pressures startup valuations and explains the rushed-to-market urgency despite CFO hesitation.
“IF I'M GOOGLE, WHY NOT TRY TO STARVE SOME OF THE CAPITAL?... TECH COMPANIES CAN CROWD OUT SOME OF THESE YOUNGER UPSTARTS IN A COMPETITIVE MOVE RATHER THAN A FINANCIAL ONE.” ▶ 43:56
The combination of giant IPOs entering indices at a time of peak passive investing creates 'blind buying' that adds a ton of market cap with little accompanying earnings.
causal🗣 Julie Biel (Kayne Anderson Rudnick)conviction: highSpaceXS&P 500Nasdaq 100
So what: Index-driven inflows pump valuations of profitless mega-IPOs without fundamental support, raising systemic valuation risk for passive holders.
“THERE'S GOING TO BE A LOT OF BLIND BUYING WITHOUT ANY REGARD TO VALUATION. SO WE ARE ADDING A TON OF MARKET CAP BUT NOT A TON OF EARNINGS.” ▶ 40:40
Execution Risk: Starship as the Swing Factor 4
SpaceX's entire future orbital-data-center vision is explicitly predicated in the prospectus on Starship working, yet Starship has done only 12 tests and has never re-landed both stages.
thesis🗣 Ed Ludlow (Bloomberg Tech)conviction: highSpaceXStarshipNASA
So what: Starship is the single biggest binary risk to the stock; investors should treat rocket-launch test outcomes as material stock-moving events going forward.
“HOW MANY TIMES DID THEY SAY ALL OF THAT FUTURE VISION ON ORBITAL DATA CENTER IS COMPLETELY PREDICATED ON STARSHIP WORKING? THAT IS THE BIG SWING FACTOR IN ALL OF THIS.” ▶ 58:56
SpaceX is contractually on the hook to help NASA return humans to the Moon in 2028 via Starship, a milestone with significant unresolved technical work.
data_point🗣 Ed Ludlow (Bloomberg Tech)conviction: mediumSpaceXStarshipNASA
So what: The 2028 lunar deadline is a concrete, trackable execution catalyst (and risk) embedded in the prospectus.
The next leg of the space race is shifting from launch to in-space mobility and logistics — moving cargo to high-energy destinations like the Moon and Mars and performing on-orbit rendezvous.
framework🗣 Tom Mueller (Impulse Space founder, SpaceX employee #1)conviction: highSpaceXImpulse SpaceStarship
So what: Identifies in-space transport as the next investable layer above launch, a thesis for backing orbital-logistics startups rather than just launch providers.
≈ “SPACEX GETS YOU TO SPACE. WITH STARSHIP, THEY REDUCE THE COST AND INCREASE THE AMOUNT OF CARGO. MY COMPANY IS HERE TO TAKE THAT CARGO TO OTHER PLACES.” paraphrase
Investor confidence in the broader space economy accelerated specifically after the SpaceX IPO was announced and Musk began talking about orbital data centers.
causal🗣 Tom Mueller (Impulse Space founder)conviction: mediumSpaceXImpulse SpaceElon Musk
So what: The IPO is functioning as a confidence catalyst that eases fundraising for adjacent space startups — a private-market spillover, even if public space peers sold off.
“ONCE THE IPO WAS ANNOUNCED AND ELON STARTED TALKING ABOUT ORBITAL DATA CENTERS, WE HAVE SEEN IT ACCELERATE.” ▶ 16:50
Market Internals & Space-Sector Rotation 5
SpaceX closed up 19% at $161.11 (off an intraday high near $176, +30%) for a ~$2.1 trillion market cap, surpassing Tesla's ~$1.5 trillion to become the most valuable company in Musk's portfolio.
data_point🗣 Carol Massar / Katie Greifeld (Bloomberg anchors)conviction: highSpaceXTeslaElon Musk
So what: A 19% close above the $135 fix confirms a successful but fading debut; the fade from +30% to +19% intraday is a tell on where natural demand clears.
Despite SpaceX's surge, a space-stock ETF fell ~7% and most space names declined (34 down vs 14 up), with Virgin Galactic off 32%, as investors rotated out of smaller speculative names into SpaceX itself.
causal🗣 Tim Stenovec (Bloomberg)conviction: highSpaceXVirgin Galactic
So what: SpaceX is acting as a capital magnet/proxy for the sector (like Nvidia for AI), draining flows from smaller space stocks — a pair-trade signal to favor the leader over the long tail.
“THE DECLINE IN SPACE-RELATED STOCK UNDERSCORES HOW SPECULATIVE THE SPACE BUSINESS REMAINS, SOME INVESTORS SHIFTING ATTENTION TO SPACEX INSTEAD OF SMALLER COMPANIES IN THE INDUSTRY.” ▶ 51:51
The Russell 2000 outperformed the S&P 500 by the most in over two years (up ~0.8% on the day, ~4% on the week), driven partly by investors chasing AI exposure through lower-quality small-cap industrial and semiconductor names.
data_point🗣 Julie Biel (Kayne Anderson Rudnick)conviction: mediumRussell 2000S&P 500
So what: The small-cap rotation reflects an AI-proxy chase into lower-quality names; a caution that breadth is improving but quality of the buying is not.
Adobe fell ~6.8% despite beating quarterly estimates, with the decline driven by a CFO departure announcement amid ongoing fears that AI is disrupting legacy software.
data_point🗣 Tim Stenovec / Romaine (Bloomberg)conviction: highAdobe
So what: A beat-and-fall on a personnel headline shows the market is unwilling to give incumbent software the benefit of the doubt on AI disruption — a bearish tell for the software complex.
Roku surged ~20% on a Bloomberg report it is in talks to sell itself, with discussions held with at least one U.S. media company though no deal is certain.
data_point🗣 Romaine / Carol Massar (Bloomberg)conviction: mediumRoku
So what: An unrelated M&A pop worth watching as a streaming/media consolidation signal independent of the SpaceX story.
Space Economy & Direct-to-Retail Capital Raising 3
Roughly $31 billion has been invested in the space market so far in 2026, versus about $48 billion for full-year 2025, putting the sector on pace for a strong year, with ~40 space companies already public.
data_point🗣 Heather Pringle (Space Foundation CEO)conviction: highSpaceXSpace Foundation
So what: Concrete sizing of space-sector capital inflows; useful baseline for gauging whether the SpaceX IPO accelerates total industry funding.
“JUST THIS YEAR ALONE, THERE IS BEEN $31 BILLION INVESTED IN THE SPACE MARKET... LAST YEAR... IT WAS RIGHT AROUND $48 BILLION.” ▶ 1:06:58
Companies that have raised heavily in private markets are increasingly reaching out directly to retail investors as institutional and private-capital wells run dry, making retail the next enormous pool of assets to tap.
thesis🗣 Lise Buyer (Class V Group)conviction: mediumSpaceXGoogleSquarePinterest
So what: A structural shift toward founder-direct retail distribution in IPOs; watch for more deals bypassing traditional institutional bookbuilding to court fan-base retail demand.
≈ “SOME OF THE PRIVATE INVESTORS AND BIG INSTITUTIONS, THOSE WELLS ARE BEGINNING TO RUN A LITTLE DRY, AND WHEN ONE RUNS DRY, YOU MIGHT WANT TO TAP A DIFFERENT ONE.” paraphrase
Ice Cube's Big3 three-on-three basketball league is going public, framed as letting fans capture upside, part of a broader trend of founder-led brands marketing IPOs directly to fan-investors.
anecdote🗣 Ice Cube (Big3 founder)conviction: mediumBig3WWE
So what: Another data point in the fan-base-as-shareholder distribution trend; relevant to how sports/entertainment IPOs may bypass traditional Wall Street demand.
Wall Street Week | SpaceX Goes Public, Google’s AI Bet, World Cup Price Backlash57 min31 insights2026-06-12
▶ Watch on YouTube
A Wall Street Week roundtable covering an unprecedented wave of trillion-dollar tech IPOs (SpaceX, Anthropic, OpenAI), the US government taking $2B in equity stakes across nine quantum computing firms led by IBM, Google's AI-driven overhaul of search, and a fan backlash over record World Cup ticket pricing as FIFA targets $11B+ in revenue.

Trillion-Dollar Tech IPOs 10
SpaceX, Anthropic, and OpenAI are each going public at roughly a trillion-dollar-plus market cap, an unprecedented scale of valuation and capital raised at IPO with no historical precedent.
data_point🗣 Steven Rattner (Willett Advisors)conviction: highSpaceXAnthropicOpenAIWillett Advisors
So what: Signals a structural shift in IPO scale; investors should expect mega-cap debuts to become a recurring feature rather than one-offs.
“We've never had companies of this kind of valuation on the day they go public, go public. We've never had this amount of capital being raised in an IPO.” ▶ 1:36
SpaceX is being valued at roughly 95 times trailing revenue (not earnings), a multiple that depends entirely on future expectations rather than current fundamentals.
data_point🗣 David Westin / Steven Rattnerconviction: highSpaceX
So what: Extreme revenue multiple means downside risk is severe if growth disappoints; the valuation prices in an unbuilt future AI/data business.
“You're saying like 95 times their trailing revenue. Not earnings, revenue.” ▶ 2:40
SpaceX's launch and Starlink businesses, while excellent, are individually too small to justify a trillion-dollar valuation, meaning investors must believe in an as-yet-unproven AI/data-center-in-space or Grok-driven upside.
thesis🗣 Steven Rattner (Willett Advisors)conviction: highSpaceXStarlinkGrokElon Musk
So what: The bull case rests on speculative optionality; the durable cash-generating segments alone do not underwrite the price.
“But it's a small business, and it's never going to be a trillion dollar valuation kind of business... And so you have to believe that out there there's something else.” ▶ 4:50
Among the three mega-IPOs, profitability diverges sharply: Anthropic is already profitable, SpaceX is mixed (profitable in parts, losing in others), and OpenAI has yet to make money.
data_point🗣 David Westin / Steven Rattnerconviction: highAnthropicSpaceXOpenAI
So what: Anthropic offers a cleaner, profitable pure-play AI exposure versus OpenAI's cash burn and SpaceX's conglomerate complexity.
“And OpenAI has yet to make money. Anthropic actually is making money.” ▶ 3:21
Companies staying private much longer means a large share of upside may have already been captured pre-IPO, raising the risk that public investors buy in after the best growth is behind the company.
causal🗣 Steven Rattner (Willett Advisors)conviction: highSpaceXIPO market
So what: Late-stage IPO buyers may be late to the value creation; academic research shows smaller IPOs underperform post-listing.
Elon Musk holding roughly 85% of SpaceX voting control combined with his roles as CEO, CTO, and chairman creates significant key-man risk, though Rattner separates concentrated voting control from the value Musk personally adds.
thesis🗣 Steven Rattner (Willett Advisors)conviction: highElon MuskSpaceXGwynne Shotwell
So what: Governance and succession risk are concentrated in one person; the stock is effectively a bet on Musk's continued involvement.
“Well there's definitely what we call a key man risk. This company is a unique creation of Elon Musk.” ▶ 6:39
Circular deals between AI firms—such as Anthropic reportedly paying SpaceX about $1.25 billion a month with a 90-day out clause—echo dot-com-era dark-fiber contracts but are less risky because underlying AI demand is real.
contrarian🗣 David Westin / Steven Rattnerconviction: mediumAnthropicSpaceX
So what: Investors should scrutinize contract length and repeatability, but Rattner argues the circularity is backed by genuine revenue unlike the 1990s.
“I think like $1.25 billion a month, but with a 90 day out.” ▶ 8:17
Large-language-model providers have exceeded revenue projections by more than any businesses Rattner has seen in his career, with adoption faster than any technology in history.
data_point🗣 Steven Rattner (Willett Advisors)conviction: highAIlarge language modelsAnthropicOpenAI
So what: Unprecedented revenue beats support the bull case for AI infrastructure spend and underpin the mega-IPO valuations.
“I don't think I've ever in my career seen businesses exceed a set of projections by as much as these companies have.” ▶ 8:52
Despite enormous AI capex, there is no observable crowding-out of capital in markets—corporate bond spreads and these firms' ability to raise (mostly equity) capital show the capital markets are deeper and more robust than previously imagined.
contrarian🗣 Steven Rattner (Willett Advisors)conviction: mediumcorporate bondscapital markets
So what: Refutes the fear that AI investment is starving other sectors of capital; bond pricing shows no funding stress.
“One of the things we're learning from this period is that the capital markets are deeper, and more robust, and more fulsome than we probably ever would have imagined.” ▶ 10:39
IPO sizes ratchet structurally higher over time—from ~$20B deals (Alibaba, Saudi Aramco, GM) to the current ~$75B level—so even larger future offerings should be expected rather than dismissed as once-in-a-lifetime.
framework🗣 Steven Rattner (Willett Advisors)conviction: mediumAlibabaSaudi AramcoGeneral Motors
So what: Frames IPO scale as a secular uptrend; do not anchor on prior records when sizing future deals or underwriting capacity.
“And now we're at $75 billion. So I would never say never. I think the numbers just get larger over time.” ▶ 11:15
Government as Quantum Venture Capitalist 9
The Trump administration is taking $2 billion in equity stakes across nine quantum computing firms, with IBM receiving half of the total investment.
data_point🗣 David Westin (narration)conviction: highIBMTrump administrationquantum computingRigettiD-Wave
So what: Government equity (not grants) in quantum lifted the whole sector including Rigetti and D-Wave; IBM is the anchor beneficiary.
“IBM by itself will receive half of the government investment.” ▶ 12:29
The government taking equity stakes rather than funding infrastructure (as it did with ARPANET) is a flawed model because government historically picks operating winners poorly and lacks the multi-decade attention span quantum requires.
thesis🗣 Sam Palmisano (former IBM CEO)conviction: highIBMARPANETTrump administrationquantum computing
So what: Skepticism on government equity model; the better historical playbook is building infrastructure and letting private firms compete.
“And this is where I think the model is challenged, because when you make an equity investment... at the end of the day, you're picking.” ▶ 21:45
The $2 billion government quantum investment is trivial relative to the roughly $850 billion being invested in AI and is only a start, not the finish, of what quantum development will require.
data_point🗣 Sam Palmisano (former IBM CEO)conviction: highquantum computingAI
So what: Frames quantum as massively underfunded relative to AI; signals far more capital is needed before commercial viability.
“$2 billion is nothing. It's what it's going to take, just go through the development cycles.” ▶ 22:44
IBM targets meaningful quantum advantage and commercial payoff by 2029, while IonQ's CEO claims its machines are already 36 quadrillion times more powerful than rivals and that it is five years ahead.
prediction🗣 Jerry Chow (IBM) / Niccolo De Masi (IonQ)conviction: highIBMIonQJerry ChowNiccolo De Masi
So what: Competing timelines and metrics (total qubits vs algorithmic qubits) make the quantum leadership claim unresolved—watch which metric the market rewards.
“our machines, we announced on September 12th at our Analyst Day, are 36 quadrillion times more powerful than anyone else's machine.” ▶ 19:14
IonQ claims it can build a fully fault-tolerant two-million-qubit system with cost of goods sold under $30 million, asserting the lowest unit economics in the industry.
data_point🗣 Niccolo De Masi (IonQ)conviction: highIonQ
So what: If true, unit-economics edge is the differentiator for IonQ versus IBM's qubit-count-led roadmap; a key bull-case metric to verify.
“we're able to build a fully fault-tolerant two million qubit system and keep our cost of goods sold under $30 million.” ▶ 19:39
Google and Microsoft were excluded from the government quantum funding; Google says the funding would have come with conditions that slowed its progress.
data_point🗣 David Westin (narration)conviction: highGoogleMicrosoftIonQquantum computing
So what: Largest tech players opted out, suggesting government strings may deter the best-funded players and concentrate state capital in mid-tier firms.
“Google says that funding from the government would have come with conditions that would have slowed down its progress.” ▶ 20:21
Quantum computing will work in a hybrid architecture—a quantum machine linked by high-speed optical link to classical cloud infrastructure—handling intractable, physics-heavy problems requiring 100% accuracy that probabilistic AI cannot.
framework🗣 Sam Palmisano (former IBM CEO)conviction: highIBMquantum computingAIcloud
So what: Quantum complements rather than replaces AI; the addressable use cases are accuracy-critical domains like trading, currency, and drug discovery.
“You cannot run a trading floor with that-- with 90% correctness. You can't move currencies with 90%. You can't cure the disease and be 90% right.” ▶ 50:58
The nine-company government quantum portfolio hedges both financial risk and unresolved technology choices, with success defined as roughly four or five of nine working rather than all nine.
framework🗣 Sam Palmisano (former IBM CEO)conviction: mediumquantum computingIBM
So what: Treat quantum exposure as a venture portfolio—expect attrition and concentrate on firms solving error correction and the software/commercialization layer.
“They don't--they don't need all nine to work... if they get four or five to work, it's going to be a successful return.” ▶ 53:42
China lacks a robust VC and private-equity ecosystem and has an inconsistent track record on broad-market investment, but is genuinely strong in national-security-driven industrial policy including quantum and cyber.
contrarian🗣 Sam Palmisano (former IBM CEO)conviction: highChinaquantum computingcyber
So what: China's quantum threat is concentrated in state-directed national-security domains, not broad commercialization—shapes where US must compete hardest.

data_point🗣 Courtney (Bloomberg reporter)conviction: mediumIranUnited States

AI capex booms are temporarily accretive to S&P earnings because chip sellers like Nvidia book full revenue and profit while buyers capitalize and depreciate the expense over 5-10 years, creating a mismatch that reverses when orders are pulled.
framework🗣 Jim Chanosconviction: highNvidiaS&P 500
So what: This accounting mismatch means current AI-driven earnings are flattered; a slowdown in orders could trigger a sharp earnings drop similar to 2000-2001.
From mid-1998 S&P operating earnings rose about 30% over two years on the capex boom, then dropped about 40% from mid-2000 to mid-2001 when order books got pulled.
data_point🗣 Jim Chanosconviction: highS&P 500
So what: The 1998-2001 earnings round-trip is a quantified template for how violently AI-cycle earnings could reverse.
Insurance (hedging/downside protection) is currently cheap, which Chanos flags as notable in the current environment.
thesis🗣 Jim Chanosconviction: medium
So what: Cheap tail protection plus record issuance argues for buying hedges now rather than after volatility spikes.
“INSURANCE IS CHEAP.” ▶ 20:24
Chanos runs a long-the-market, short-a-basket-of-bad-businesses strategy (hedged since 1996, now running own money), which has stayed profitable even as indexes hit new highs because many individual stocks have lagged dramatically.
framework🗣 Jim Chanosconviction: high
So what: A relative long/short structure can profit from dispersion even in a bull market, useful for positioning around overvalued IPO names.

Social Security solvency and US fiscal path 5
The latest trustees report indicates Social Security old-age benefits will run short around 2032 (and the combined old-age, survivor and disability trust fund around 2034), implying an across-the-board benefit cut of roughly 22% if nothing is done.
data_point🗣 Jack Lew (former Treasury Secretary)conviction: highSocial SecurityBrookings Institution
So what: A legislated or automatic ~22% benefit cut by the early 2030s is a concrete fiscal and consumer-spending risk that markets and retirees should price in.
“A 22% BENEFIT CUT IF NOTHING HAPPENS.” ▶ 30:44
The US spends about 23% of GDP while raising about 20% of GDP in revenue, the persistent ~3-point gap that drives the federal deficit, and any durable fix must address taxes as well as spending.
data_point🗣 Jack Lew (former Treasury Secretary)conviction: highUS federal budget
So what: The structural spend/revenue gap frames why bond supply keeps rising and why fiscal sustainability depends on politically hard tax increases.
A viable Social Security fix requires a long lead time because benefit and revenue changes take years to take effect, so action must come before the end of the next president's first term rather than be deferred to 2032.
framework🗣 Jack Lew (former Treasury Secretary)conviction: highSocial Security
So what: The lead-time constraint compresses the real decision window, raising the risk of disorderly last-minute fixes if Congress delays.
Lew favors raising the Social Security earnings cap (taxing more of high earners' wages) as a relatively easy policy lever, while noting the system already does modest redistribution through progressive benefits.
thesis🗣 Jack Lew (former Treasury Secretary)conviction: mediumSocial Security
So what: Lifting the earnings cap is the most likely revenue fix, with implications for high-income payroll tax exposure.
Growing debt-service burden as a share of the federal budget will crowd out spending on priorities from defense to anti-poverty programs, with Medicaid and SNAP already cut deeply, making poverty programs more politically vulnerable than universal Social Security.
causal🗣 Jack Lew (former Treasury Secretary)conviction: highMedicaidSNAPUS federal budget

How a Vibecoded Newsletter Is Making the Hay Market More Transparent | Odd Lots40 min24 insights2026-06-12
▶ Watch on YouTube
Aiden Johnson, the 20-year-old founder of Haywire, explains how he and a co-founder used AI/vibe coding to turn opaque USDA hay auction data plus phone-call 'ground intel' into a plain-English pricing newsletter. The conversation surfaces a structurally opaque, hyper-local, $8B alfalfa market under acute drought-driven supply stress, and the playbook for monetizing neglected public data.

Hay market structure and opacity 5
The US hay market is not a single market but a collection of fragmented, hyper-local regional markets because high transport costs prevent moving hay across the country.
framework🗣 Aiden Johnsonconviction: highhayalfalfaHaywire
So what: Pricing signals and trades must be evaluated region-by-region; there is no national benchmark to arbitrage against, so local supply/demand dominates.
“it's not just one market. Um, it's like fragmented markets across the whole country. Every market, every regional market is very hyper local” ▶ 15:08
Transporting hay typically costs $5 to $8 per mile via brokers and dealers, which can easily exceed the value of the hay itself over distance.
data_point🗣 Aiden Johnsonconviction: highhaybrokersdealers
So what: Transport economics cap regional price arbitrage and explain why coastal/eastern markets stay insulated from western shortages; a natural ceiling on price convergence.
“these brokers, these dealers will charge anywhere from $5 to $8 per mile to have it shipped. So, that can easily average to be more valuable than the hay.” ▶ 17:49
There is no agreed benchmark hay price; the USDA NASS publishes only average costs, and some Midwest participants use Rock Valley auction data as their de facto regional benchmark.
data_point🗣 Aiden Johnsonconviction: highUSDA NASSRock Valleyhay
So what: Absence of a reference price is the precondition that blocks a hedgeable, financialized hay market — the whitespace Haywire is trying to fill.
“Rock Valley specifically is in the Midwest, um, use their auction data as the benchmark of kind of what hay is going for.” ▶ 14:57
Deals in the hay market are often struck blindly by phone with no reference price, e.g. a buyer simply asking '$157 per ton' and the seller agreeing, leaving margins arbitrary.
anecdote🗣 Aiden Johnsonconviction: highhaybrokers
So what: Illustrates the raw informational inefficiency a transparency layer monetizes — and why incumbents resist it.
“can I just do $157 um per ton for, you know, x amount of bales or tons? And he was like, yeah, yeah, that works. So, we're not going off anything here. There's no transparency.”
Middlemen in commodity markets, including hay brokers, are the participants who lose most from greater price transparency because they profit from making deals blindly.
causal🗣 Aiden Johnsonconviction: mediumhaybrokersmiddlemen
So what: Identifies who will resist transparency tools and where margin compression lands as data products penetrate opaque commodity chains.
“the middleman will always feel the u out or the outcomes of more transparency because they're the ones making deals u especially in the hay market kind of blindly and kind of just guessing” ▶ 35:30
Hay supply stress and drought 5
46% of US alfalfa acreage is currently in drought, concentrating supply stress in western states.
data_point🗣 Aiden Johnsonconviction: highalfalfawestern US
So what: A hard supply-side stress metric supporting a bullish near-term price view for hay/alfalfa and watchpoint for downstream feed costs.
“46% of the US alphalfpha acreage is actually in a drought.” ▶ 17:05
First-cut hay yields are coming in extremely light and 2025 carryover stocks are essentially gone, with buyers stockpiling ahead of summer.
data_point🗣 Aiden Johnsonconviction: highhayranchersfarmers
So what: Light first cut plus zero carryover signals tightening supply into summer — a setup for continued price spikes.
“I'm hearing that first cut is being extremely light. the yields are not really producing at a volume and carryover from 2025 is essentially gone.” ▶ 18:35
USDA average hay prices rose to roughly $180/ton in April (from $167 in March), with alfalfa around $185/ton (from $175 in March).
data_point🗣 Aiden Johnsonconviction: highUSDAhayalfalfa
So what: Concrete month-over-month price levels confirming the upward trend; usable reference points for tracking the move.
“right now, I'm looking at hay for April, it's $180 per ton, which is up $167 from March. And then for the alpha alpha side of things, it's $185 a ton, which is up from $175 since March.” ▶ 14:14
Western drought may be pulling demand eastward, a pattern Haywire dubbed the 'Missouri pattern' after observing Missouri prices rise, then Rock Valley (Iowa) prices spike in subsequent weeks.
thesis🗣 Aiden Johnsonconviction: mediumMissouriRock ValleyIowahay
So what: A leading-indicator framework: watch western Midwest auction prices to anticipate demand migration and price contagion eastward.
“we saw demand in or prices in Missouri go up... then the next week we saw um Rock Valley's prices kind of spike a little bit” ▶ 22:13
A farmer pulled hay back from auction because tightening demand made him expect prices to rise further and he wanted to keep the supply, signaling hoarding behavior.
anecdote🗣 Aiden Johnsonconviction: mediumhayfarmers
So what: Withholding supply amid expected scarcity is a self-reinforcing dynamic that can accelerate price spikes.
“he actually pulled it back because the demand for hay was getting so increasingly like it was getting really tight and he needed the hay. So, he's kind of predicting it to get even tighter.” ▶ 24:42
Hay quality and downstream demand 4
Hay quality is graded by Relative Feed Value (RFV) on a base of 100, with roughly 150+ considered high quality; protein resides in the leaf and fiber in the stem, and rain during curing lowers protein and quality.
framework🗣 Aiden Johnsonconviction: mediumRFVhayalfalfa
So what: RFV is the key quality metric segmenting the market; weather during harvest directly determines which buyer tier (dairy/equine vs beef) the hay serves.
“RFV. a relative feed value... it's a base of 100. So anything like 150 I think is pretty good.” ▶ 25:20
Demand is segmented by buyer: dairies and horse owners buy high-RFV alfalfa/supreme hay while beef cattle take lower-quality utility hay, and dairies blend high and low RFV to control rations and stretch volume amid high prices.
framework🗣 Aiden Johnsonconviction: mediumdairieshorsesbeef cattlealfalfahay
So what: Price stress flows differently by segment; blending behavior by dairies is a demand-side shock absorber worth watching when premium prices spike.
“these dairies are going to be the ones buying the higher quality RF alalfa to um you know, feed these cows that are producing 80 pounds of milk a day. And they're actually taking that high higher quality RF and then mixing it with lower quality RF” ▶ 24:09
Higher hay prices are raising second-order costs such as horse boarding fees and pushing equine operations toward unprofitability, with a Texas horse sanctuary reportedly near closure due to hay and gas costs.
causal🗣 Tracy Alloway / Aiden Johnsonconviction: mediumhayhorsesTexas
So what: Demonstrates cost pass-through from feed commodities into adjacent service costs and potential demand destruction in the equine segment.
“We love our horse so much, but the return on investment on horses right now is just like it's too it's too expensive.” ▶ 23:50
Alfalfa covers part of roughly 49-50 million acres of US hay acreage and is an ~$8 billion-per-year crop, ranking as the fourth most valuable US crop.
data_point🗣 Aiden Johnsonconviction: highalfalfahay
So what: Quantifies the market's scale — large enough to justify dedicated data products and a potential futures market despite its opacity.
“you have hay acorage covering 49 to 50 million acres across the US and itself, alalfa itself is producing 8 billion dollars per year, which is the fourth most valuable crop.” ▶ 30:53
Land use competition: data centers, solar, crops 4
Data centers built on agricultural land compete with alfalfa for acreage, and if sited in drought-prone western states their cooling could compete with water-intensive alfalfa for water.
thesis🗣 Aiden Johnsonconviction: lowGoogledata centersalfalfawestern US
So what: A non-obvious long-term supply constraint linking AI infrastructure buildout to feed-crop supply and water scarcity in the West.
“alalfa is already a very water intrusive plant. So then alalfa is going to have to be competing with these big data centers for water.” ▶ 28:28
Google is building a 482-acre data center near Jamestown/southern North Dakota that will be air-cooled, illustrating direct conversion of agricultural land to data infrastructure.
data_point🗣 Aiden Johnsonconviction: mediumGoogledata centers
So what: Concrete local example of farmland-to-datacenter conversion pressuring crop acreage.
“Google is actually going to build a 482 acre data center 20 minutes south of me” ▶ 27:46
Landowners face four main options for idle acreage: rent to farmers per acre, enroll in USDA conservation (which locks up land access for 25-35 years), lease to solar developers for large payments, or grow the highest-value crop.
framework🗣 Aiden Johnsonconviction: mediumUSDAsolarfarmland
So what: Maps the competing economic uses determining how much acreage stays in hay/crop production versus energy or conservation.
“the USDA will basically pay you to conserve it and then the the downside of that is you probably lose your land or access to your land for 25 to 35 years.” ▶ 29:15
Solar developers are paying farmland owners large sums to install panels, with whole farms in the Northeast already converted entirely to solar fields.
data_point🗣 Aiden Johnson / Tracy Allowayconviction: mediumsolarfarmlandNortheast US
So what: Solar leasing is an active competing land use removing acreage from agricultural production, tightening crop supply.
“these solar panel companies are coming in to maybe place solar panel panels on your fields um for a huge chunk of change” ▶ 29:29
AI-enabled niche data business model 6
A durable business can be built by finding free public data nobody regularly watches, aggregating it into a central repository, and becoming the recognized authority ('the axe') who comments on it.
framework🗣 Joe Weisenthalconviction: highHaywireFlightradar24USDA
So what: A repeatable playbook for monetizing neglected public datasets across many opaque markets — the core investable thesis of the episode.
“find a piece of public data that exists that is out there that is consumable for free that nobody is regularly looking at gather it become the central repository for it and then occasionally comment on it” ▶ 3:22
AI and vibe coding make it newly economical for two people to build a market-data company that would previously have required a large team, unlocking niche markets too small to serve before.
thesis🗣 Aiden Johnson / Joe Weisenthalconviction: highAIvibe codingHaywire
So what: The cost collapse in data tooling expands the addressable universe of viable micro-data businesses — a structural AI productivity unlock.
“it makes it able for, you know, two young 20-year-olds like me and my co-founder Cole to, you know, mess around and create a whole company and platform.” ▶ 13:08
Haywire combines a direct USDA API feed of weekly auction prices with phone-based 'ground intel' from auction houses, cross-referencing the two to validate data and filter out errors or 'hallucinations.'
framework🗣 Aiden Johnsonconviction: highHaywireUSDARock Valley
So what: The triangulation method — official data plus human calls — mirrors how illiquid benchmarks like LIBOR and scrap metal were historically priced.
“we get live feed strictly from them and that basically funnels into our four system type of I guess you could say verification” ▶ 5:49
Haywire monetizes via a freemium newsletter, with a pro tier at $14/month rising to $17/month after June 30, plus a potential API/data-licensing business line.
data_point🗣 Joe Weisenthal / Aiden Johnsonconviction: highHaywire
So what: Low price point means scale or low headcount is essential; API licensing is the higher-margin path that could expand the business beyond newsletters.
“It's just $14 a month... the price of being a founding member is going to rise to $17 a month after June 30th.” ▶ 37:43
Older-generation farmers are reluctant to adopt technology, making distribution and onboarding the hardest part of building a transparency product in agriculture.
causal🗣 Aiden Johnsonconviction: mediumfarmersHaywire
So what: Adoption friction, not data collection, is the key go-to-market risk for ag-data startups; networking and free data-sharing offers are the workaround.
“especially these old gen farmers, they're not really going to adapt to technology like that. So it's been kind of uh difficult trying to get them to adapt” ▶ 20:34
A tradable, hedgeable hay market cannot exist until an agreed reference index is established, and hay's extreme weather-driven volatility is precisely what makes a hedging instrument valuable yet hard to standardize.
thesis🗣 Joe Weisenthal / Aiden Johnsonconviction: mediumhayfuturesexchanges
So what: Whoever establishes the credible hay benchmark could enable hay futures — a large optionality on top of the data business.
“you can't have a hedgeable tradable financialized market until you first have that index, right? something that everyone agrees on as a reference price” ▶ 33:35

Lawfare Daily: How Escalations in Lebanon May Prolong the Iran War, with Joel Braunold56 min31 insights2026-06-12
▶ Watch on YouTube
Joel Braunold argues that Israel's inability to refrain from hitting Hezbollah without triggering Iranian retaliation has 're-linked' the Lebanon and Iran fronts, undermining Israel's core war objective and making a US-Iran nuclear deal less likely before Israel's September 2026 election. He frames Israeli electoral incentives, a fracturing US-Israel relationship in both US parties, and fragile Lebanese politics as the key variables that keep the Iran war grinding on with the Strait of Hormuz still in play.

Front Linkage and the Beirut Red Line 4
Israel's central strategic objective is to keep the Lebanon and Iran fronts de-linked, so that an Iranian strike cannot be triggered by Israeli action against Hezbollah; the current de facto status quo where Israel cannot strike Beirut without an Iranian response represents a strategic failure for Israel.
thesis🗣 Joel Braunoldconviction: highIsraelIranLebanonHezbollahBeirutTehran
So what: Re-linkage means the Iran conflict has no clean off-ramp; any flare-up in Lebanon can reignite direct Iran-Israel exchanges, keeping a tail risk on regional energy and shipping that a Hormuz-watching investor should track.
“Jerusalem cannot afford for there to be a linkage of the fronts. DC doesn't really care” ▶ 9:09
A workable but fragile equilibrium has emerged: Israel may continue operations against Hezbollah across southern Lebanon, but Beirut itself is ring-fenced, and so long as Israel does not strike Beirut, Iran will not strike Israel.
framework🗣 Joel Braunoldconviction: highIsraelIranHezbollahBeirutSouth Lebanon
So what: The 'Beirut trigger' is the single most watchable escalation line; a confirmed Israeli strike inside Beirut would likely precede renewed direct Iran-Israel missile exchanges and a spike in regional risk premia.
“If Beirut's off limits, that's okay.” ▶ 8:22
Iran demanded Israel halt operations in South Lebanon as a condition for ceasing fire, but Israel did not stop and Iran did not retaliate, revealing that Iran's real red line is Beirut specifically, not South Lebanon.
causal🗣 Joel Braunoldconviction: highIranIsraelSouth LebanonHezbollah
So what: Iran's revealed (not stated) threshold tells traders where the genuine escalation line sits, letting them discount South Lebanon noise but treat Beirut strikes as the actionable trigger.
“Israel has not stopped their operations in South Lebanon and Iran has not responded” ▶ 15:00
The Trump administration created a direct Beirut-to-Jerusalem ceasefire negotiating track to keep Lebanon's ceasefire owned by the Lebanese government rather than by the IRGC or Tehran.
data_point🗣 Joel Braunoldconviction: mediumTrump administrationBeirutJerusalemIRGCTehranLebanese government
So what: Empowering Beirut as the counterparty is the diplomatic mechanism that could either stabilize or collapse the ceasefire; its success determines whether the Lebanon front stays contained.

Trump publicly told Israeli media outlets (Barack Ravid and Channel 11) in advance that he would order Netanyahu not to strike Iran, then Netanyahu struck anyway, demonstrating the limits of US leverage to restrain Israeli retaliation once Iran has fired on Israel.

The Nuclear File and the Shape of a Deal 5
For Israel the nuclear file is the overriding priority ('A, B and C'), ranking far above proxies and ballistic missiles, and a deal that leaves Iran able to retain enriched uranium or enrich domestically would be considered a bad deal.
framework🗣 Joel Braunoldconviction: highIsraelIranJCPOA
So what: Defines the precise term that makes or breaks the deal for Israel; any agreement permitting domestic enrichment will draw maximal Israeli spoiler behavior, lowering the odds of durable de-escalation.
“nuclear file is definitely A B and C and then the other things go under that” ▶ 21:28
The closure of the Strait of Hormuz is not materially relevant to Israel's economy because Israel relies on its own eastern Mediterranean natural gas fields and is not a significant exporter through the Strait, making it a 'Gulf problem' from Israel's perspective.
contrarian🗣 Joel Braunoldconviction: highIsraelStrait of HormuzEastern MediterraneanGCCGulf
So what: Because Israel is insulated from a Hormuz closure, it lacks the economic incentive the US and Gulf states have to settle quickly, so energy-price pressure will not force Israeli concessions on its timeline.
“the threat of the straits of Hammouth being closed is not that relevant for them” ▶ 18:26
Repeated US strikes are still hitting Iranian radar arrays and anti-aircraft systems that were previously declared destroyed, casting doubt on official claims about how degraded Iran's military assets actually are.
contrarian🗣 Joel Braunoldconviction: mediumUnited StatesIran
So what: If Iranian air defenses keep regenerating, the conflict is less degraded and more protracted than headlines suggest, supporting a longer war-of-attrition base case.
“every time that we think that we've been told all of this stuff has been destroyed suddenly it's back again” ▶ 19:58
Iran is seeking war-damage reparations and Strait-of-Hormuz-related concessions as part of any deal, terms Israel views as unacceptable, widening the gap to an agreement.
data_point🗣 Joel Braunoldconviction: mediumIranIsraelStrait of Hormuz
So what: Iranian maximalist demands plus Israeli red lines make a near-term deal unlikely, keeping the conflict and its energy-risk overhang in place.
“The Iranians want reparations from the war damages, straits of Hammuz, all this stuff.” ▶ 24:14
Israel's ultimate policy goal is a weakened Iran leading to regime change, and Israel would not object if Iran splintered into a failed state, whereas the GCC and even the UAE fear an Iranian failed state and are seeking an equilibrium that keeps the regime contained but standing.
thesis🗣 Joel Braunoldconviction: highIsraelIranGCCUAESaudi Arabia
So what: The divergence between Israel (wants collapse) and the Gulf (wants stable equilibrium) means Gulf states will quietly work to prevent the very outcome Israel seeks, a fault line that shapes which regional deals get backed.
“for the Israelis anything that leaves the Iranian regime standing is bad” ▶ 21:21
Israeli Election as the Binding Timeline 4
Israel will hold elections between September 15 and September 27, 2026, and the political incentive for Netanyahu is to prevent any US-Iran deal that strengthens the Iranian regime until after the election, making no deal preferable to a deal in this window.
prediction🗣 Joel Braunoldconviction: highIsraelNetanyahuIran

Scaling Laws: Lawyering on the Frontier with Janel Thamkul48 min16 insights2026-06-12
▶ Watch on YouTube
Janel Thamkul, who built Anthropic's Frontier Legal Team after leading legal for Google Research, argues copyright law is the wrong tool for AI's real problems (economic displacement and incentives to keep creating), and that the underserved frontier issues are job-displacement safety nets, AI's energy/environmental supply chain, and AI's impact on cognitive development. She frames frontier AI lawyering as a translation layer between researchers and regulators that must push legal boundaries without 'yolo' ignoring precedent.
Copyright vs. AI: the real fight is elsewhere · 5Underpriced frontier risks: jobs, energy, cognition · 4Frontier AI legal function as competitive infrastructure · 5Practitioner playbook: low-ego curiosity as an edge · 2
Copyright vs. AI: the real fight is elsewhere 5
Copyright law is the wrong legal instrument for the AI-and-creators dispute; the real underlying issue is economic displacement, which is broader than copyright and will hit lawyers, financial professionals, and many industries, not just creatives.
thesis🗣 Janel Thamkulconviction: highAnthropiccopyright lawAIcreative industries
So what: Signals a frontier-lab legal view that copyright litigation against AI labs is misframed and that the durable policy battle will shift to economy-wide displacement remedies; watch for labs steering settlements/policy away from copyright precedent toward broader compensation schemes.
“I think economic displacement is an issue that's broader than copyright. It's broader than creatives.” ▶ 44:22
Copyright-based remuneration claims are not aligned with how content is actually used to train a model, making copyright a poorly-fitted 'hammer' for one niche of a much larger problem.
causal🗣 Janel Thamkulconviction: highcopyright lawmodel trainingAI
So what: A former Anthropic frontier lawyer arguing training use is mechanistically distinct from copying supports labs' fair-use posture; relevant to litigation risk pricing for AI labs and to rights-holders' leverage.
“I don't think it is aligned with how content is actually used in training a model.” ▶ 44:45
The second, distinct policy problem is preserving incentives for humans to keep creating; the concern (echoing Judge Chhabria in the Meta case) is not that Taylor Swift is harmed but that future Taylor Swifts won't write songs if AI dilutes the market.
framework🗣 Janel Thamkulconviction: highJudge ChhabriaMetaTaylor SwiftcopyrightAI-generated content
So what: Separating 'displacement compensation' from 'creation incentives' reframes where regulation and lab policy will land; investors in generative-media and licensing platforms should watch for incentive/subsidy mechanisms rather than pure copyright enforcement.
“this is going to impact all the future Taylor Swifts and make future Taylor Swifts not write songs um because AI will you know dilute the market” ▶ 45:37
Stock-photography-style commercial creators face genuine economic displacement from generative AI, whereas creators who don't depend on art for livelihood are largely insulated because their value is in the process, not the output.
thesis🗣 Janel Thamkulconviction: mediumstock photographygenerative AIcreators
So what: Identifies commoditized commercial content (stock imagery) as the most exposed creative segment to AI substitution; a sector-level short/avoid signal versus process-driven or brand-driven creative work.
“someone who is like making a living off of generating stock photography is in a very different position” ▶ 46:43
Google Books is the seminal copyright precedent for AI training, and outcomes are playing out as anticipated even before ChatGPT.
framework🗣 Janel Thamkulconviction: highGoogle BooksAnthropiccopyrightfair use
So what: Reinforces that AI-training fair-use defenses lean on Google Books; a key anchor for assessing litigation exposure of any model developer using copyrighted corpora.
“in AI for copyright in particular Google books is you know seinal precedent that you have to know and we're seeing it play out uh in ways that we anticipated” ▶ 32:02
Underpriced frontier risks: jobs, energy, cognition 4
Society is behind on building social safety-net systems for AI-driven job displacement, and the standard 'upskilling/re-education' playbook is hollow because it doesn't define what value the displaced worker actually gains.
thesis🗣 Janel Thamkulconviction: highAI automationjob displacementsocial safety netsbig tech layoffs

“Oh, upskilling, uh, you know, re, you know, re-education so that people understand how to use AI. To what end” ▶ 39:33
AI's compute buildout depends on supply-chain and energy inputs with significant community and ecosystem impacts, and the US is behind on clean energy and on the environmental rules needed to balance AI energy demand against climate and local impacts.
thesis🗣 Janel Thamkulconviction: highclean energydata centersAI supply chainenvironmental regulation

A distinct and underdiscussed risk is AI's impact on cognitive development for middle- and high-schoolers who are still learning how to think critically, as opposed to its effect on already-skilled adults.

Anthropic was about 100 people when Thamkul joined in 2023, and she built out its entire legal function (commercial, product counsel, litigation and regulatory, plus interim employment counsel and patent prosecution) before specializing the team on a Frontier AI Council.

In a field where AI development changes daily/weekly/monthly, senior lawyers' reluctance to admit knowledge gaps becomes a liability; institutionalizing a 'hall pass' for new hires to re-ask how training is done keeps the team current.
framework🗣 Janel Thamkulconviction: highfrontier legal teamAI governance
So what: A management insight for fast-moving technical domains: seniority that discourages questions degrades accuracy; relevant to how legal/compliance teams keep pace with model development.

Oil shock, inflation and Fed path 7
The Iran-war oil price spike did two damaging things at once: it reversed an expanding 52-week-high list (killing the broadening trade) and raised inflation expectations, knocking the Fed off its easing path and kneecapping housing, financials, real estate and insurance stocks.
causal🗣 Josh Brownconviction: highIranoilCPIFederal Reservehousingfinancialsreal estateinsurance
So what: If oil reverses, these blown-up rate-sensitive sectors are positioned to lead the next leg; trade the reversal.

Levitt watches the 5-year breakeven inflation rate, not headline CPI; coming into the year it sat near 215 bps, moving toward 265 is worrisome, and it is currently back around 250, signaling price stability and no rate hikes.
framework🗣 Brian Levittconviction: high5-year breakevenbond marketFederal ReserveCPI

≈ “you have a 5-year break even, probably around 215... you move to 265 or so, that starts to get a little bit worrisome. We're back at 250.” paraphrase
Today's inflation is a price shock from oil, not the wage-driven inflation of four years ago, which is why Levitt does not expect Fed rate hikes and views the cycle as able to continue as long as price stability holds.
thesis🗣 Brian Levittconviction: highFederal Reserveoilinflation
So what: Distinguishes a transitory supply shock from sticky wage inflation; supports staying invested rather than de-risking on CPI prints.
“We have the price shock... That was the problem we had four years ago. We don't have that today.” ▶ 20:08
A historical trigger for cycle-ending tightening is the 5-year breakeven breaking above 3%; once that happens banks tighten lending standards and credit spreads blow out, and that is not the current environment.

The knockout-punch fallacy 6
The average investor wrongly expects a single news-event 'knockout punch' to start a bear market, but the real knockout punch (e.g., Lehman) arrives only after a 25-30% drawdown is already underway — Lehman failed because of the bear market, not the reverse.
framework🗣 Josh Brownconviction: highLehman Brothers
So what: Reframes risk: don't position for a headline catastrophe from a market high; the dangerous failures cluster after deterioration is visible.
“that knockout punch comes at the bottom... By the time Leman goes under, they went under because of the bare market.” ▶ 29:05
Market tops form gradually — good news fails to lift stocks, a rally retests but undercuts the prior high, six months pass without a new high, enthusiasm fades, a 10% drawdown deepens, and the Citi economic surprise index rolls over before any final blow.
framework🗣 Josh Brownconviction: highCiti economic surprise index
So what: Gives a concrete checklist of topping behavior to monitor instead of reacting to single scary headlines.
“The top will look like this. There'll be great news and stocks won't react well to it.” ▶ 31:41
Bears have predicted the same valuation-driven 'Big Bang' top for years, rotating the trigger from Silicon Valley Bank to tariffs to the Iran war to the SpaceX IPO; Levitt argues indicators flashing red for three years are simply the wrong indicators.
contrarian🗣 Brian Levittconviction: highSilicon Valley BanktariffsIranSpaceXShiller PE
So what: Persistent false signals (valuation, margin) should be deprioritized; focus on cycle indicators that actually move ahead of trouble.
“if they've been flashing around for three years, maybe they're the wrong indicator” ▶ 59:00
Levitt's actual cycle-analysis toolkit is leverage, inflation expectations, Fed policy, credit spreads, bank lending standards and dollar strength — and explicitly excludes PE ratios and strategist price targets.
framework🗣 Brian Levittconviction: highcredit spreadsbank lending standardsUS dollarFederal Reserve
So what: A usable, named indicator set for cycle risk; PE only matters for 10-year return forecasts, not timing.
“I look at leverage, inflation expectations, Fed policy, credit spreads, bank lending standards. That's my cycle analysis.” ▶ 59:13
The economy is 18 years from its last genuine recession (excluding COVID), and the persistent search for a knockout punch over that span has been a costly distraction from staying invested.
thesis🗣 Josh Brownconviction: highCOVID
So what: Argues the opportunity cost of perpetual bearishness is enormous; bias toward staying invested through scares.
“We're 18 years from the last recession. So for 18 years, people are looking for this knockout punch” ▶ 30:51
The genuine risk is a Minsky moment in which complacency itself becomes the danger, not the geopolitical or valuation scares investors fixate on.
framework🗣 Brian Levittconviction: mediumMinsky moment
So what: The real tail risk is the absence of fear; monitor sentiment extremes rather than headline shocks.
“that's the Minsky moment where people become so complacent that that becomes the risk”
Investor positioning, leverage and momentum 6
Margin debt is best evaluated as a percentage of M2 money supply rather than dollar terms, and elevated readings are not a timing tool because margin naturally rises with the market and falls within roughly three months of even a 10% drawdown.
framework🗣 Ben Carlson / Josh Brownconviction: highmargin debtM2Liz Thomas
So what: Dismiss margin-debt scare headlines as timing signals; it mean-reverts naturally without a crash.
“the market pulls back 10% and stays down... that margin balance comes down... Naturally, it doesn't it's not a nuclear bomb going off.” ▶ 27:16
Rising margin debt is increasingly driven by wealthy investors and advisors borrowing against highly appreciated portfolios to deploy into the real economy, making it a weaker risk signal than in the past.
thesis🗣 Josh Brownconviction: highmargin debt
So what: Structural change in who uses margin undermines its value as a froth indicator.

Consumer, K-shaped economy and household strength 5
US household net worth is about $175 trillion at an all-time high with liabilities-to-net-worth historically low, making household fundamentals far stronger than widely believed.

Because of the K-shaped economy, S&P 500 earnings are driven by spending from the top income quintiles; a slowdown in the bottom quintile dents activity but barely touches index earnings, and $4.50 national gasoline hurts low earners without hurting the broad market.
framework🗣 Brian Levittconviction: highS&P 500K-shaped economygasoline

Bitcoin 200-week MA and bottom timing 7
Bitcoin has wicked below its 200-week moving average (tagging $59,000) for the first time this bear cycle since November, recovering back above it to around $62,000.
data_point🗣 David Hoffmanconviction: highBitcoin
So what: The 200-week MA is the key technical line traders are watching as a potential bottom signal; a sustained break below historically only occurred during extreme contagion events.
“Bitcoin tagged $59,000 which is firmly below it. It has since recovered to above it. First time below the 200 week in this bare cycle since November” ▶ 3:46
Bitcoin has only meaningfully camped below its 200-week moving average once in history, during the 2022-2023 FTX/Three Arrows Capital contagion combined with the fastest rate hikes in Bitcoin's history.
framework🗣 David Hoffmanconviction: highBitcoinFTXThree Arrows Capital
So what: Frames the bull case: without a comparable contagion event or rate shock, a sustained break below the 200-week MA is historically unlikely, suggesting a bounce is more probable.
Crypto is experiencing its third 25% drawdown since November, the deepest yet, having round-tripped the entire Trump presidency back to Biden-era Bitcoin prices.

Jim Grant: The Next Crash Won't Look Like 200865 min40 insights2026-06-12

Jim Grant argues AI is one of the greatest capital-spending bubbles of all time, comparable to the 1870s railroad buildout, and that the Fed is now structurally constrained from fighting inflation because zero-rate-era leverage in private credit, private equity and life insurance makes higher rates dangerous. AI capex bubble vs historical analogues 7
AI is one of the greatest financial bubbles of all time, with inflation-adjusted dollars at stake exceeding the late-1990s internet/fiber buildout and the role of the Fed being more intrusive than then.
thesis🗣 Jim Grantconviction: highAIFederal ReserveSpaceXOpenAIAnthropic
So what: Signals elevated crash risk in AI-levered equities and credit; positions a contrarian short or underweight against consensus AI euphoria.
“So I think that today is one of the greatest bubbles of all time.” ▶ 4:01
There is likely substantial overbuilding and double-ordering of AI capacity (semiconductors, data centers) just as occurred in the late 1990s, because better information technology does not restrain the human speculative spirit.
causal🗣 Jim Grantconviction: highAIdata centerssemiconductors
So what: Watch for a demand-miscalculation bust in chip and data-center supply chains analogous to the 2001 telecom glut.
“I think there's a great deal of overbuilding, uh, double ordering, just like, you know, there was in the late 1990s.” ▶ 3:30
The best historical analogue for AI is the railroad buildout, not fiber optics, because the capital draw on AI is as great as it was on railroads while the value proposition remains far more uncertain.
framework🗣 Jim Grantconviction: highAIrailroads
So what: Frames AI as a capital-intensive infrastructure cycle whose returns may disappoint even if the technology succeeds; useful lens for sizing exposure.
“What we do know is that the capital draw on AI is as great as it was on the railroads.” ▶ 8:06

data_point🗣 Meb Faberconviction: mediumSpaceXOpenAIAnthropic
So what: Quantifies the scale of late-cycle issuance supply that could saturate demand and mark a top.
Sectors that become very dear can collapse to extreme cheapness in short order: utilities reached a CAPE near 60 in the 1920s while railroads fell to a CAPE of roughly two after the Depression.
data_point🗣 Jim Grantconviction: highutilitiesrailroadsRobert ShillerCAPE ratio
So what: Historical reminder that today's high-multiple AI leaders carry asymmetric downside; mean-reversion risk is severe.
The Dow industrials did not reclaim their 1929 high until 1954 and the rails/transports not until 1965-66, showing post-bubble recoveries can take 25-plus years.
data_point🗣 Jim Grantconviction: highDow Jones Industrialstransportsrailroads
So what: Cautions investors that buy-and-hold recovery after a major bubble can span decades, not years.
Transformative technologies need not be as capital-consuming as AI or railroads; air conditioning was as transformative as anything yet its 1950s stocks traded at only four to eight times earnings.
framework🗣 Jim Grantconviction: mediumair conditioningCarrier Corp

Deflation, inflation and monetary discipline 8
What the Fed labels deflation is often simply progress; the 1873-era decline of prices 2-3% per year for two decades was driven by human progress and creative destruction (Suez Canal, rail, steam, telegraph), not a credit collapse.
framework🗣 Jim Grantconviction: highFederal ReserveSuez Canal
So what: Reframes 'good' productivity-driven deflation versus dangerous credit-collapse deflation, helping investors distinguish benign from malign price declines.
A dangerous financial deflation is the collapse of the price level owing principally to a collapse in credit, which by definition entails major bank failures and credit destruction, unlike the benign technology-driven deflation of the railroad era.
framework🗣 Jim Grantconviction: highFederal Reservebanks
So what: Identifies the specific deflation type worth fearing, tying the risk to bank and credit solvency rather than falling prices per se.
Bernanke did investors a disservice by single-mindedly invoking the 1930s to justify suppressing the funds rate in 2009-2012, since the 1940s-50s Fed under Martin worried about inflation even during periods of negative year-over-year CPI without anyone calling it deflation.
contrarian🗣 Jim Grantconviction: highBen BernankeWilliam McChesney MartinFederal ReserveCPI
So what: Challenges the entire post-2008 deflation-fighting rationale that anchored zero rates and QE.
The defining feature of the current monetary world is that prices only ratchet up and never ratchet down, so even low 1-2% inflation leaves the prior price-level jump permanently embedded.
thesis🗣 Jim Grantconviction: highCPIinflation
So what: Explains persistent consumer discontent despite 'low' inflation prints and supports a structurally rising cost-of-living thesis.
“That's the foremost feature of our monetary world today is only rationing up, never ratcheting down.” ▶ 17:12
Inflation is fundamentally 'too much money' rather than too much money chasing too few goods; where the excess money shows up is variable, appearing in cappuccinos, New York real estate, stocks, or farmland at different times.
framework🗣 Jim Grantconviction: highinflationFederal Reserve
So what: A mental model for spotting asset-price inflation (e.g. equities, farmland) that official CPI misses, guiding where bubbles form.
≈ “Sometimes it comes up in your cappuccino. Sometimes it's in real estate in New York. Sometimes it's in stocks like now.” paraphrase
An AI data center reportedly bought farmland at $300,000 an acre, illustrating that price-insensitive buyers can radically reprice an asset relative to the marginal traditional user.
anecdote🗣 Meb Faberconviction: mediumAIdata centersfarmland
So what: Flags AI-driven demand spilling into real assets like land and power; a watch-item for farmland and adjacent real-asset valuations.
Excessive stock-market valuations and leverage are themselves a species of inflation, a qualitative view largely abandoned by today's quantitatively-driven Fed econometricians.
framework🗣 Jim Grantconviction: highFederal ReserveWilliam McChesney Martin
So what: Argues current easy financial conditions are themselves inflationary, undercutting the case for rate cuts.
Multiple current indicators point to easy money and speculative excess: margin debt has roughly doubled (or up ~50%) in the past year and the Chicago Fed Financial Conditions Index reads easy, arguing against rate cuts.
data_point🗣 Jim Grantconviction: highmargin debtChicago Fed Financial Conditions IndexFederal Reserve

The Fed is structurally constrained against an aggressive attack on inflation because of extreme financial leverage in private credit, life insurance and corporate balance sheets, a constraint it does not publicly acknowledge.
thesis🗣 Jim Grantconviction: highFederal Reserveprivate creditlife insurance

framework🗣 Jim Grantconviction: highprivate equityFederal Reserveinterest rates

Ethena's pivot from basis trade to yield curator 10
The crypto basis (cash-and-carry) trade has collapsed during this bear market, making it unattractive and pushing Ethena to reallocate toward stablecoin lending and other instruments.
causal🗣 Luke Leisureconviction: highEthenabasis tradecash and carry

“during this bear that the basis on crypto has just collapsed and that cash and carry trade really isn't too attractive” ▶ 26:19
Basis-trade yields have compressed across successive bull periods (around 20% post-Trump election, then ~15%, then ~8-10% in the latest cycle) because the trade is being commoditized as more institutions and professionals run it.
data_point🗣 Kunalconviction: highEthenabasis trade

Ethena realistically has only three scalable capital-deployment options: institutional lending, AAA CLOs, and existing DeFi lending strategies (boosted by incentives on venues like Mega ETH or Plasma), while gold and equity basis trades are too small to matter.
